LEVENSHULME v. MACCLESFIELD (Second Elevens).-At Macclesfield. Levenshulme: R. N. Heaton 31, W. Heaton 8, J. Siddall 20, H. B. Smith 6, P. Wharton 6, H. Craig 14, H. F. Lewis 5, H. Ward 1, M. Hobday 10, Foster 2, Dean 3, extras 5; total 109. Macclesfield: Wright 4, Shatwell 15, Seel 14, Lees 8, Sheldon (not out) 13, Fowler 2, Sawyer (not out) 2, extras 8; total (for five wickets) 66.

STOCKPORT V. WERNETH.
Stockport met Werneth, on the ground of the latter, for the first time this season before a grand gate of over two thousand spectators, and in charming weather. Both teams were well represented. Werneth were minus the services of Wharmby, one of their pros, but had Brookes, of the Manchester staff and late of Stockport, assisting them, and who did very well too, taking five wickets for just over nine runs a piece. The Werneth captain being fortunate in wining the toss sent in Redfern and Haggas to face the bowling of Brown and Fred Torkington. Both men scored a boundary each off Torkington, but the score had only reached 22 when Haggas was grandly caught by Harry Royle at square leg, 22-1-10. Hall, the popular Werneth pro, came next, but after scoring a single he was magnificently caught at slips by F. Tyler, 26-2-1. Heyworth followed in and here some good cricket was seen. Both men played well, though Brown troubled them with his trickiness with the ball. At 46 a change of bowling was tried, F. Tyler going on for F. Torkington. The separation, however, came from the other end, Sam Brown bowling Heyworth with a grand ball, after that player had scored 14 in nice style, 53-3-4. Wickets after this fell fast, Sam Brown being almost unplayable, the whole side being dismissed for the not over grand score of 98. This was due to the splendid bowling of Sam, whose analysis read - 21.1 overs for 8 maidens 25 runs and 7 wickets. The fielding of the Stockport men was also very good, all the catches that were took being very fine. Stockport opened their venture by sending in W. Hartley and J. Bates to the bowling of Hall and Brookes. The start was at once disastrous, losing Bates and Arthur Torkington with only 15 runs on the board. Brown and W. Hartley soon after fell victims, and four good wickets were down for 27 runs, but when Harold Hartley and Disney got together a different complexion was put on the game, and they treated the bowling with the utmost confidence, especially the former, the fifth wicket falling at 65, Disney being bowled. Fred Long next joined Hartley, who was playing a splendid game, and he also found a good partner in Long, who also played very well. Hartley, after playing a splendid game, was most unfortunate in playing on. After making 27 he was loudly cheered on returning, the sixth wicket thus falling at 89. Little needs further comment except the batting of Fred Long and Frank Tyler, the former scoring 14 and the latter 12, not out; the innings closing for 110-Stockport winning by just a dozen. It was a grand match, and was fought out right to the end very determinedly. Great praise is due to Harold Hartley, Fred Long, and Frank Tyler for the splendid way they kept their wickets up against the fine bowling of the tricky Hall and Brookes; and Stockport have every reason to be proud of their handsome victory, every man being a thorough trier. Below is appended full score and analysis.

STOCKPORT V. WERNETH (2nd Elevens).-These two old rivals met for the first time this season at Cale Green Park, in glorious weather, and before a fair attendance. The visitors, winning the toss, decided to bat a little after three o'clock, to the bowling of Jones and McLachlan. Jones is a splendidly with a fast ball, but on the whole runs came slowly, eight wickets being down for 93, when Marshall and Watson became partners and a splendid stand was made, the score being taken to 170 before either were separated, the score being still the same when the innings closed. After the usual interval Stockport commenced to bat at a quarter past five, with a rather formidable task before them. May soon set to work in earnest. The first wicket compiled 47 towards the number, May, the retiring batsman, accounting for 43 of these. F. Selby, the new man in, seemed rather uncomfortable at first, but he was getting more at home when he lost the service of Royle, the score reading 73-2-19. Marshall joined Selby with almost a hundred runs wanting, and the hand of the clock pointing to six. They had much to do, but the batsmen soon set to work, Marshall hitting four 4's in one over. Excitement ran high as time crept on. The spectators became quite riveted to the game, especially the junior portion, who were enthusiastic with delight as each batsman sent the leather to the boundary. It was a treat to watch Marshall play each fresh bowler. Nothing seemed to come amiss, but another wicket fell before the required runs were reached, Selby being cleaned bowled by the captain for a very useful 46. With ten minutes left to get the remaining nine runs, Jones joined Marshall, the latter soon making the winning hit with a boundary. He was shortly afterwards bowled for a grand 57, comprising 13 fours and 4 twos. Time arrived when the fifth wicket fell, Stockport being victorious by 11 runs and five wickets. Werneth was very strongly represented. This was their first defeat of the season; but the homesters have to come yet. Next week Stockport 2nd journey to Cheetham Hill, and it is to be hoped the team will be as successful as they were on Saturday. Score:-Werneth: H. Jackson b McLachlan 33, F. Dixon b Jones 4, J. LEVENSHULME v. MACCLESFIELD (Second Elevens).—At Macclesfield. Levenshulme: R. N. Heaton 31, W. Heaton 9, J. Siddall 20, H. B. South 6, F. Wharton 0, H. Clegg 14, C.F. Lewis 3, H. Ward 1, H. STOCKPORT V. WERNETH.
Stockport met Werneth, on the ground of the latter, for the first time this season before a grand gate of over two thousand spectators, and in glorious weather. Both teams were well re-presented. Werneth were minus the services of Wharmby, one of their pros., but had Brookes, of the Manchester staff and late of Stockport, assisting them, and who did very well too, taking five wickets for just over nine runs a piece. The Werneth captain being fortunate in winning the toss sent in Redfern and Haggas to face the bowling of Brown and Fred Torkington. Both men scored a boundary each off Torkington, but the score had only reached 22 when Haggas was grandly caught by Harry Royle at square leg, 22—1—10. Hall, the popular Werneth pro, came next, but after scoring a single he was magnificently caught at slips by F. Tyler, 26—2—1. Heyworth followed in and here some good cricket was seen. Both men played well, though Brown troubled them with his deliveries with the ball. At 46 a change of bowling was tried, F. Tyler going on for F. Torkington. The separation, however, came from the other end, Sam Brown bowling Heyworth with a grand ball, after that player had scored 14 in nice style, 53—3—4. Wickets after this fell fast, Sam Brown being almost unplayable, the whole side being dismissed for the not over grand score of 98. This was due to the splendid bowling of Sam, whose analysis read—21.1 overs for 8 maidens 25 runs and 7 wickets. The fielding of the Stockport men was also very good, all the catches that were took being very fine. Stockport opened their venture by sending in W. Hartley and J. Bates to the bowling of Hall and Brookes. The start was at once disastrous, losing Bates and Arthur Torkington with only 15 runs on the board. Brown and W. Hartley soon after fell victims, and four good wickets were down for 27 runs, but when Harold Hartley and Disney got together a different complexion was put on the game, and they treated the bowling with the utmost confidence, especially the former, the fifth wicket falling at 55, Disney being bowled. Fred Long next joined Hartley, who was playing a splendid game, and he also found a good partner in Long, who also played very well. Hartley, after playing a splendid game, was most unfortunate in playing on. After making 27 he was loudly cheered on returning, the sixth wicket thus falling at 89. Little needs for further comment except the batting of Fred Long and Frank Tyler, the former scoring 14 and the latter 12, not out; the innings closing for 110—Stockport winning by just a dozen. It was a grand match, and was fought out right to the end very determinedly. Great praise is due to Harold Hartley, Fred Long, and Frank Tyler for the splendid way they kept their wickets up against the fine bowling of the tricky Hall and Brookes; and Stockport have every reason to be proud of their handsome victory, every man being a thorough trier. Below is appended full score and analysis.

STOCKPORT V. WERNETH (2cd Elevens).—These two old rivals met for the first time this season at Cale Green Park, in glorious weather, and before a fair attendance. The visitors, winning the toss, decided to bat a little after three o'clock, to the bowling of Jones and McLachlan. Jackson opened splendidly with a four, but on the whole runs came slowly, eight wickets being down for 93, when Marsland and Watson became partners and a splendid stand was made, the score being taken to 170 before either were separated, the score being still the same when the innings closed. After the usual interval Stockport commenced to bat at a quarter past five, with a rather formidable task before them. May soon set to work in earnest. The first wicket compiled 47 towards the number, May, the retiring batsman, accounting for 43 of these. F. Selby, the new man in, seemed rather uncomfortable at first, but he was getting more at home when he lost the service of Royle, the score reading 73—2—19. Marshall joined Selby with almost a hundred runs wanting, and the hand of the clock pointing to six. They had much to do, but the batsmen soon set to work, Marshall hitting four 4's in one over. Ex-citement ran high as time crept on. The spectators became quite riveted to the game, especially the junior portion, who were enthusiastic with delight as each batsman sent the leather to the boundary. It was a treat to watch Marshall play each fresh bowler. Nothing seemed to come amiss, but an-ther wicket fell before the required runs were reached, Selby being cleaned bowled by the captain for a very useful 46. With ten minutes left to get the remaining nine runs, Jones joined Marshall, the latter soon making the winning hit with a boundary. He was shortly afterwards bowled for a grand 57, comprising 13 fours and 4 twos. Time arrived when the fifth wicket fell, Stockport being victorious by 11 runs and five wickets. Werneth was very strongly represented. This was their first defeat of the season; but the homsters have to come yet. Next week Stockport 2nd journey to Cheetham Hill, and it is to be hoped the team will be as suc-cessful as they were on Saturday. Score:—Werneth : H. Jackson b McLachlan 33, F. Dixon b Jones 4, J. CHESHIRE COUNTY NEWS: FRIDAY 2 AUGUST 1895 - 2

SATURDAY'S MATCHES.
Castleton v. Stockport.
This match should have been played at Castleton, but owing to the ground being unfit caused through the heavy rains it was cancelled. Next week the Stockport team journey to Bolton, to try conclusions with the town club.

ST. THOMAS' ATHLETIC v. NORBURY 2ND.—At Norbury. The home team batted first, scoring 14 all out. J. Bowden took seven wickets for five runs, and W. Burt three for eight. J. Garner and J. Bowden went in to bat for St. Thomas'. They had scored 28 when rain stopped the match, leaving St. Thomas' winners by 10 wickets and 14 runs. STOCKPORT AND DISTRICT SHIELD
COMPETITION.
FINAL TIE.
STOCKPORT CONGREGATIONAL v. REDDISH ST. ELISABETH'S.—This interesting event, for a beautiful shield presented by Mr. Sydney Hollins, was commenced on the football ground adjoining the Stockport Cricket Ground on Saturday afternoon. The pitch, which had been prepared for the occasion, was saturated by the rain, and all in favour of the bowlers. The Congregational have vanquished the Lads' Club and St. Peter's, whilst for St. Elisabeth's position they have had to meet and defeat the Lads' Club 2nd and last year's winners, Hanover. The Congregational batted first, sending in Dowbiggin and S. Jebson, they having to face the bowling of Hardman and Hunt. Both batsmen hit well. The innings only realised 49. Reddish started with Hardman and Dyson, Lee and Hardy looking after the bowling. Runs came pretty freely. Score:—Congregational: Dowbiggin b Hunt 6, S. Jebson b Hardman 4, Jebson run out 4, Hardy b Hunt 6, Cheetham b Hardman 1, Beaty c Saxon b Hardman 2, Lee b Hardman 1, Ridley b Hardman 0, Hague b Hardman 1, Barratt not out 6, Hardy b Hunt 6, extras 2; total 49. Reddish St. Elisabeth's: Hardman b Hardy 5, Dyson lbw b Lee 20, Grimshaw c Hague b Lee 0, Miller b Lee 6, Raynor c Barratt b Lee 0, Hunt c Jebson b Hardy 2, Mason b Dowbiggin 3, Shawcross not out 1, Burrows not out 3, Saxon and Lomax did not bat, extras 2; total for seven wickets 42.

Stockport v. Little Lever.
This match was played at Little Lever in splendid
weather and before a large attendance of specta-
tors. A start was not made till quite three o'clock
owing to the Stockport men being delayed on the
road. The match was set apart for the benefit of
Penny, one of the Little Lever professionals, and
one who has done splendid service with the ball,
this being his first year with them. Stockport still
deplored the services of Fred Torkington and one
or two others, while Little Lever had their
strongest team in the field this season.
Little Lever won the toss, and electing
to bat sent in Towler and Tyldesley to the
bowling of Brown and F. Tyler. Tyldesley had a
splendid reception on coming out—a fact due, no
doubt, to that player being admitted to the Lan-
cashire County ranks, and it will be remembered
that on his second appearance for his county he
scored no fewer than 153 not out against Warwick-
shire. Brown opened to Towler, beating him first
ball, and the next ball snicked him leg stump. A.
Smith came next, but after scoring six in a wretched
fashion Brown upset his wicket. C. Smith, the
Lancashire County wicket-keeper, joined Tyldesley,
but immediately after Tyldesley was splendidly
taken by Bates right on the boundary, and three
good wickets were down for seven. Pool came next,
and soon began to force matters, hitting Brown
out of the ground for four. We may say the ground
is a very small one indeed, it being no trouble to
get a ball out. Smith seemed content with
singles. The score rose steadily, and a change of
bowling was tried at 30, Harry May displacing
Tyler; the change almost worked immediately,
May bowling Pool for a well hit 20. Hallows came
next, and got May away to leg nicely for three,
but at the other end Brown bowled him with a
grand ball, and half the wickets were down for 45
runs. Simm joined Smith; the latter with his
score at 16 was missed by Bates at long off. Simm
was the next to go, Brown upsetting his wicket.
61—6—0. Knutton followed, and hit up 11
in four hits, but was then beautifully taken by
Harold Hartley off Brown. 65—7—11. Northrop
came next, and bore a little stand was made,
Northrop getting May away for three while Smith
cut the same bowler for a like number. The score
had reached 81 when Brown disturbed Smith's
wicket for a lucky innings of 22. A strange misun-
derstand piece during Smith's innings, and one
which certainly did not reflect much credit on the
Little Lever umpire. W. Hartley, who had dis-
placed May, was bowling his last ball of his second
over, Smith hit the ball cut to Bates at square leg
and was caught, the umpire, in the meantime,
saying "over," and when Bates had caught Smith
the umpire give it a "no ball," and thus Smith was
allowed to proceed with his innings. The end soon
came, the innings closing for 91. Sam Brown
deserves great credit for his splendid bowling,
taking no fewer than eight wickets for 37 runs,
hitting the stumps six times, while the other two
were caught. Stockport opened their venture in a
dreadful bad light. J. Bates and W. Hartley being
the first batsmen to face Knutton and Penny.
Bates after scoring a single was caught at the
wicket. A. Torkington joined his captain, but after
scoring five snicked the last of Bates. Brown came
next, and hitting one out to Tyldesley was splendidly
caught and three wickets were down for 11 runs.
Rain then commenced to fall, and put an end to all
further play, and what would have been an in-
teresting finish was spoiled. The Stockport team
fielded quite up to form. On Saturday next Wer-
neth are down at Cale Green, and this match is set
apart for the benefit of Sam Brown and Jim Disney.
As this is one of the best fixtures on the card and a
fixture which takes well with the Stockport people,
the two "pros." should materially benefit by it.

CRICKET MATCH AT HAZEL GROVE.
A cricket match was played on the Hazel Grove Cricket Ground at Norbury on Monday afternoon, between Hazel Grove and Poynton. For several years past these two rival clubs have contested for supremacy, and considerable interest was taken in the match. In consequence of the annual Wakes a considerable number of people were enabled to be present and evinced a lively interest in the proceedings. The Hazel Grove Brass Band was in attendance and played lively selections of music during the afternoon. During the earlier part of the afternoon the rain fell in torrents, and it was thought that the match would have to be abandoned. At three o'clock, however, the time fixed for the commencement of the match, the rain cleared off, and was succeeded by brilliant sunshine. Both elevens were fairly representative of the two clubs. Poynton, however, had a strong team, while Hazel Grove were short of the services of Gledhill, Penney, and Wood. The home captain won the toss and elected to bat. Cleaver and Fallows were the first to wield the willow, to the bowling of Rhodes and Clarke. Play was for a time rather dull, runs coming very slowly. Cleaver, the first man in, was neatly caught when he had succeeded in knocking up 8, Fallows' stay was very short, a solitary one falling to his lot; and Standring's career was cut short before he managed to break his duck. Roberts and Penney played well together, the latter especially batting steadily. Roberts, although he played a cautious game, was only successful in obtaining four runs, Wadsworth, with a neat catch, compelling him to relinquish his bat. Only about 30 runs had been scored at four o'clock, while five wickets had fallen, and the prospect for the home eleven began to look rather gloomy. Penney, who was playing a good game, was now partnered by Daniels, who lost no time in breaking his duck, and by united efforts these players speedily succeeded in raising the score marked on the indicator to 40. Daniels sent a beauty to leg for two, which was speedily followed by a three. His good luck, however, now came to an end, and the next ball took his sticks. The rest of the Hazel Grove team were quickly disposed of. Smith and Clayton were the first to take their stand against the bowling of Standring and Bennett. It was soon evident to the spectators that Poynton's chances of winning were rosy indeed. The Poynton lads batted well, and notwithstanding the good fielding of the homesters, quickly increased the score.
Hazel Grove 53, Poynton 33. The top score for Poynton was 25, made by A. Clayton. W. Bailey came second with 24 to his credit.

CHESHIRE COUNTY NEWS : FRIDAY 23 AUGUST 1895 - 2

CRICKET.
SATURDAY'S MATCHES.
Stockport v. Werneth.
STOCKPORT WIN BRILLIANTLY.
This match between these old rivals was played at Cale Green Park in magnificent weather, and before a large crowd of spectators, it being also set apart for the benefit of the professionals, Brown and Disney, who throughout the season have done splendid work, and it is hoped that they will receive a substantial reward. It will be remembered that the first time these teams met at Werneth this season Stockport won a most interesting match by eleven runs, due on the whole to the splendid bowling of the evergreen Sam Brown. On Saturday skipper Hartley won the toss, and of course chose to bat on a splendid wicket, taking in with him J. Bates to face the bowling of Hall and Hodgkinson. The start was none too good from a Stockport point of view, as Bates in trying to get a full toss from Hodgkinson to leg was tragically caught at slip. Arthur Torkington joined his captain who cut Hall splendidly for four, but after scoring ten he was caught at the wicket. Sam Brown came next and was warmly greeted by the crowd. He immediately got to work, scoring much faster than Torkington. The score had reached 37 when Torkington was caught by Redfern, after that player had scored six. Disney joined his brother professional but only to see him brilliantly caught at short slip by Collinson, after playing a splendid game. E. M. Bromley-Davenport of Cheshire county and Cambridge University fame came next, and here a splendid stand took place, both men defying all the efforts of the Werneth bowlers. Davenport delighted the spectators with his big hitting, while Disney, on the other hand, was playing a most finished game, his cutting being a treat. At 72 Davenport was run out in stepping forward to a fast ball from Seanor, which hit the wicket keeper's pads and then came back into the wicket before Davenport could recover his ground, he had hit up 17 in a very few minutes, and he was loudly cheered on retiring. F. Tyler joined Disney, but shortly after the latter was caught at mid off for a splendidly played innings of 21. The telegraph board now reading 77-5-21. F. Selby came next, but little needs for further comment with the exception of Frank Tyler's display, which was a really meritorious performance. His innings of 44 not out included six four's, two three's, and three two's. He was ably assisted by J. Brewster, who played a nice innings of eleven before being caught on the boundary. The innings closed for the respectable score of 139. After the usual interval Werneth opened their venture by sending in Wharmby and Eaton, the attack being entrusted to Sam Brown and Bromley-Davenport. Brown opened with a maiden over, but Eaton got the amateur's first ball away for three, but soon after lost Wharmby, who was nicely caught by Selby at cover point, the first wicket falling at nine. Napper took Wharmby's place but with an addition of six runs lost Eaton, that player being defeated by a grand ball from Brown. Redfern was next in and next out, being beaten first ball by a "yorker" off Davenport, and three wickets were down for 15 runs. Matters never altered much for with the exception of Heyworth, Hall, and Seanor, no one seemed able to play the bowling, the innings closing for 70, Stockport thus being credited with a win over Werneth for the second time this season. I must just make a note of the splendid bowling of Sam Brown and Harry May, the former taking four for 18, and the latter four for 15 and this along with the splendid fielding of the Stockport men, who never missed a chance, was due in no small measure to their brilliant victory. The spectators seemed to fairly enjoy the afternoon's sport, and especially so to see their own men come out on the right side.

BIRKENHEAD ADVERTISER: SATURDAY 24 AUGUST 1895 - 1

CRICKET.
BY UBIQUE.

RESPECTED SIR,-Far be it from me to deny, or even deprecate, the gingerbread-making qualities of that marvellous place, Ormskirk; but, sir, I must gently but firmly draw the line at what the Ormskirk natives glorify by the name of cricket. Cricket, forsooth! I--Eh? Where's Ormskirk? You don't mean to say you never heard of Peerless Ormskirk? Dear, dear! What appalling ignorance! Why, you can find it on the map with a microscope and a week to spare-
Oh! Ormskirk is a marvellous spot;
A marvellous spot it be.
And woe to the man who says it's not:-
(With bullets of gingerbread he'll be shot)-
For the natives can point on the map to a blot
As big as the eye of a flea!
And it boasts a standing population of a cricket (?) team, seven enthusiastic spectators, and a small dog. Oh! and also a gent. what scores for the team and makes speeches with an average of 45 perorations about the paralysing miracles of batting with which the players weekly astound the world as it stares gaping and transfixed with wonder and awe. What's that? Well, if you don't believe me ask the select audience which was grouped within and around the scoring box last Saturday, and which went black in the face with suppressed laughter as the Petrifying Puffer held forth during the progress of the fiasco between Ormsquirk and the Park.

Yes, sir! Fiasco is the right and only term to apply to the occasion,-none other genuine. For I do not speak without serious consideration when I protest that of all the many and various samples of the noble art of batting I have seen, never-I emphatically repeat-never have I experienced so insufferably feeble and unworthy an exhibition as that of Ormskirk on the Park's ground last week end. Faugh! I scarce have patience to think of it; -of the hundreds at the match not one spectator with a spark of manliness or sportsmanship in him, could look at the farce without a sneer of contempt or a disgusted exclamation. And the remarks of the scornful crowd were numerous and varied, but very much to the point. But let me rouse your indignation by a short description of the thing. In the first place, to show there was no ill-feeling, the Park lost the toss; which, considering Ormskirk's somnolent methods, was fatal. For when Ormskirk goes to the wickets and begins to "bat," the spectators are liable to fall into a refreshing sleep. Though, to speak fairly and give them their due, I have known an Ormskirk player, under favourable circumstances, to make a two hit! But one cannot always expect such a feat.

Well, the gingerbread purveyors, with great assiduity commenced to block every ball that toddled up the pitch and the scoring for an hour averaged upwards of one run to each over! But what price this, -Ormskirk took an hour to score their first 30 runs?? We had to pour cold water down the Park-scorer's neck to keep him awake,-he wasn't used to it. All sorts of bowlers were tried, but it was block, block, block, and nothing came of it, but frantic yells of delight from the crowd when one of the stone-waller's scored a single! Which was a rare occurrence. As time dragged wearily on, and maiden overs were painfully fashionable, I could stand it no longer, and went for a walk to have a quiet swear to myself. When I returned-joy! the last Ormskirk stick in the mud was bowled, and I then discovered that the whole eleven had positively and actually taken up 3 (three!!) hours in scoring a total of 149 runs! I muttered a prayer of thanksgiving that it was all over, and washed it down with a No. 9 Pipkin.

You may bet your respectability that Holden and Co. were just itching to lay their hands on the bat, and show these timid Ormskirkers how runs were made. And, by the Hokey!-but stay; all in good time. As the gingerbread gents had very carefully contrived to leave the Park three-quarters of an hour in which to bat, our premsers could not expect to give their opponents the all-powerful thrashing they administered at the last meeting. So Ormskirk had gained their end. Well, Holden and Ould opened the cannonade, and I may say at once that the captain's phenomenal display of gigantic hitting has rarely, if ever, been equalled on the Park ground,- which is a large order! Bennion and Barnes were the trundlers to suffer. I wept for them. Thirty-four runs in five overs! Four upon four came from Cecil's wonderful strokes, and at one time he followed up four successive boundary hits by swiping the ball clean over the pavilion for 6! What a paralysing contrast to Ormskirk's senile pottering at the crease! For, though but 30 minutes at the wickets Holden piled up 51 runs, which, with the exception of five singles, was one mass of 4's with a 6 thrown in for the sake of variety. With apologies to "The Man that Struck O'Hara,"-

First he spanked 'em through the crowd,
Then he banled 'em where he chose;
Then he had another go-
Smiting sixes fours and twos!
Such a sight you never saw :-
(Ormskirk but for time would lose)
Rat-tat-tat!
A whirling bat,
A high-skied ball
Was left-that was all-
Of poor Ormskirk's gruelled bowling!
And the sanguine scorer who made speeches said "Well, I never did!" and tried to change the subject. When time arrived the Park had totalled 75 in three quarters of an hour, and having six wickets in hand, nothing come of it-to the visitors' relief. Of course, had there been time to win the home eleven would have adopted steadier tactics, but Cecil always knows when to do the right thing, more power to him. And in awakening the crowd sleeping under the influence of the Ormskirk-narcotic, he did the greatest thing of all. Ormskirk! Bah! I'd strike 'em off the fixture list, eh W-?

When Oxton tried conclusions with Huyton, at Oxton, on Saturday, a Liverpool criticaster remarks, "small scoring was the order." Of course, it's purely a matter of taste, but though "small scoring" was the order for Oxton and Huyton, some degraded people would prefer their order to be small Skotch! Not too small, but just large enough. [The Local Vetoist will please excuse this culpable outburst of Humanity.] Huyton, batting first, fell away, so to speak, on the spot. Their experience was indeed a sad one, for the Bowling Bishop and Smith relieved them of their duties by the time the total had hobbled up by jerky stages to 55. Oxton smiled largely as they proceeded to knock 'em off. But Oxton are sanguine persons, and you never can tell. For our suburbans manifested an alarming propensity for hatching "ducks," and wanted-very badly!-about 10 runs to win when the ninth wicket went by the board. But-dear old "but"!-amid tremulous excitation and agitated gaspings, P. Brownell and W. Scott saw the sinking ship safe through the breakers into harbour (pardon the simile), and when the last man was "potted" Oxton had reached the winning post first by 7 runs. Those similes have got a bit mixed up somehow, so we had better escape to Aigburth.

At Aigburth, on Saturday, Rock Ferry were busy replying to Liverpools' challenge. But they were more busy with the ball than the bat, for the castigation they had to submit to covered the whole ground, and so did the South End fielders, many times over. People said that after such a thrashing the hearts of the Rocks would be pained and sore, but all practical persons will agree with me that after such a "burst" of fielding, the soreness would be located in their feet. I merely offer this as an 'umble opinion, and as a guarantee of good faith. However, Fax is what we're hankering after. Rock Ferry opening the batting, immediately parted with the wickets of Kripps, Ravenscroft, and A. Stott, two of whom would get their l.b.w. It was a bad business. This ominous decay was delayed by Needam (32) and T. R. Bretherton (15), but the remainder of the eleven, "not to be denied," had a little rot "on their own," which was so successful that the grand total recorded was 67. Not very dazzling, was it? By the way, Needham was also out l.b.w. Three leg-before decisions are enough to drive an eleven into an asyl-er-hotel. Minus the discomfort of having to face E. C. Ryland-who would have done them a world of good on such a wicket-Liverpool enjoyed themselves, as I have hinted, and the fun waxed fast and-faster (Ha, ha! caught you tripping there, eh?) and when hostilities ceased they had piled up 174 for 7 wickets. The only South End bowler to make an impression was A. Stott, who captured 6 of the 7 wickets for only 39 runs. If Ryland had been there to help him, who can tell what-but "ifs" are idle now!

As I witnessed part of the contest between Victoria and New Brighton, at the Park, on Saturday, I certainly expected the Vics to annex what I thought would be a well-merited triumph. Instead of which they were beaten by 31 runs, so that New Brighton have avenged the unhappy downfall they underwent when the season was young. A trifle more of that estimable cricketing virtue, consistency, would have given the homesters the palm, for they could unfortunately only produce three double-figurers, viz:-J. McDonald-the substantial old warrior-(45), J. Wylde (25), and G. Lumgair (16). Only this and nothing more. And I would respectfully inquire of such hitters as W.T., J. Brocklebank, Tom Fish, &c., why this unwarrantable thusness? Now, on the other hand, the visitor's distribution of duty gives one a sensation of satisfaction when looking at their list, as the double-figurists were seven strong. Yes, "we are seven."

At last! our Police 1st eleven have had to knuckle under and sing exceedingly small; though Brombro Pool on Saturday surely "made them dance." The contest, which was witnessed at the Pool, was a surprise and no mistake. In this way. The home eleven-who are famously erratic in mixing up,-

Great deeds, and doings small,
Other deeds and none at all!
with the assistance of J. Pugh (44 not out), and the Rev. F.B. (20), totted up 123. The boys in "white" promised to cover this in double-quick time, A. Wright and J. Howard-each 20 runs-commencing the visitors' retaliation in brisk fashion. But the ways of the Brombro Bowlers are curious and inscrutable, and lo and behold! likewise ecco! not another policeman could stand before them; the remaining 9 rotted to a man, and the constables' feelings were hurt when they suddenly discovered that they were beaten by 33 runs!

A nameless individual writes :-
Dear Sir,-Referring to the remarks in your cricket notes on Saturday last re the cricket match Bromboro Pool v. Birkenhead Victoria, I notice, as on many previous occasions, you do not give the Poolites the credit that is due to them. You state that the Pool had first turn at the wickets, which was a considerable advantage. Now this is not the case, as the Victoria had the first use of the wicket, and being that you attribute their downfall partly to them having to bat second, it makes the Pool's victory all the more meritorious. Hoping for the future you will be a little more correct in your reports,      INTERESTED.
The italics are mine. I am not surprised that "Interested" had not sufficient manliness to enclose his name and address; as it is quite in the nature of things that a person who can stoop to such untruthful allegations, can only be expected to strike in the dark. As to my last Saturday's mistake, as Bromboro' Pool never trouble to send me the reports of their engagements, I must obtain information from whatever other source is available; and if an error thus creeps in, the Pool have only themselves to thank. As to the anonymous snarl that on "many previous occasions" I have not given the Pool due credit, if my hidden gentleman will step out into the light like an honest man, drop generalities for facts, and name his "occasions," I shall be able to deal with him as he deserves.

As I was not able to see the Park v. Victoria engagement on Wednesday, I insert the following remarks of a correspondent:- "The Park, batting first was a queer team, made 110. In reply the Vics. had made 60 for 3 wickets, when Holden went on to bowl. He was also knocked about for a time, but, changing ends, he put 'em in with terrible effect, and upset the last 6 wickets for 8 runs!" The Vics totalled 81. Comment would be useless.

New Brighton's meeting with Liverpool on Wednesday proved disastrous, for in reply to the Liverpudlians 239 they could only notch 58!

Cheshire went down again this week, being beaten by Worcester to the tune of 7 wickets, though the scoring all round was low.

On Saturday, Mr. Wynne's team thrashed Mr. Griffith's team by 64 to 47, the top-scorer being F. Nicholls (20) for the "Wynners!" Scores only received yesterday. - Six days after the event-much too late for insertion.

Cricket in Wirral on Saturday, was plentifully bestrewn with insignificant contributions. For instance. Neston and District had a bout with Kirkdale Wesleyans, and thrashed them by 99 to 46. Dr. Speechless-beg pardon, Speechly (22) being the shining star. - Again at Port Sunlight-the Cheshire "Sweet Auburn"-the Soapees had totalled 55 and placed four Helsby champions hors de combat for 30 runs when the game terminated.

Spital, on their own ground on Saturday, did a big thing, for Boughton Hall were just saved from a smart whipping at their instigation. The home exponents gave a tasty display of solid and consistent batting, which ended in a rather large 193 for only 8 wickets; and they had followed this up by clearing off six of the Hall gents. for a pigmy 77 when time interfered, and the contest ended in what some people-at Chester-call a draw. Hardly!

On Monday and Tuesday last Woodland played their last match, when they were opposed by the Kingsley, whom they whacked by 3 wickets and 9 runs. Woodland throughout the season have:-
Played. Won. Lost. Drawn.
  29      22      7     0
Which I think is very good for a junior club.

A correspondent sayeth:-"The return match between St. Mary's and Tranmere Wesley was played at Woodchurch-road on Saturday, the home team being somewhat late in arriving. The Saints-as distinct from the "Wesleyans!"-batted first, two wickets at once falling for 3 runs! Matters, however, soon changed, Linekar, Sault, Gaskell, F. Williams and S. Jenkins getting well into double figures, and completely mastering the bowling, Gaskell in particular hitting with characteristic vigour. With one man short, the total reached 92. Tranmere Wesley started well, T. Francis and Buckley getting into double figures, but the remainder gave little inconvenience, and time alone saved them -due to the late start-from defeat, 8 wickets having realised only 62 runs." Oh, Time, Time! you have a heap to answer for!-The proceedings of the second

STOCKPORT CHRONICLE : MONDAY 26 AUGUST 1895 - 2.

SATURDAY'S MATCHES.
Stockport v. Cheadle Hulme.
Great interest was manifested in the meeting of these local rivals, which took place on the Cheadle Hulme ground in splendid weather and before a good attendance of spectators on Saturday afternoon. It was also set apart as the benefit of the Cheadle Hulme professional, Teather. The first match between these elevens this season ended in a very even draw. Skipper Hartley lost the toss, and of course had to field. C. V. Rome and Teather were the first batsmen to open the Cheadle innings to the bowling of Sam Brown and H May. Teather scored a single off Brown, but next over Harry May knocked his leg stump out of the ground. E Rome came next, and immediately cut Brown for four, while C Rome got May away for a like number, but was out soon after, Brown taking him well at short slip. 12-2-5. Briggs joined E. Rome, but with an addition of eight runs on Rome was finely caught by Hartley after scoring 10. A. Royle was next in, and saw Briggs get Brown away to leg for four, and next over repeated the strike very luckily. Royle hit May for three, but soon after that player was magnificently caught by Harry May at extra slip low down-a very fine catch indeed. I. Taylor joined Briggs, and here a stand took place, though Briggs did most scoring. At 62, however, Fred Torkington, who had displaced May, clean bowled Briggs, that player having made 27 in not a very taking fashion; thus half the wickets were down for 62. W. Redfern came next, but without any addition Fred Torkington upset his middle stump with one of his expresses. Taylor left at 67 and Rome at 69, while Kirk left at 71, there being nine wickets down. Then came a surprise for the Stockport men. Todd and Colles on getting together made things very lively for the fielders. Changes of bowling were tried, but to no use, the 100 going up amidst loud applause; both men played a fair game. Todd several times getting the ball away in nice style. W. Hartley went on with lobs, but caused no great sensation. When the total had reached 127 Colles was bowled by Fred Torkington, his innings of 15, coming at a time when things were looking serious, was of the utmost value to his side, while too much cannot be said of Todd, who carried out his bat for a splendid innings of 34. Certainly the Stockport men assisted them materially by their wretched fielding, but nevertheless they deserve great credit for their plucky partnership. Fred Torkington came out with a splendid analysis out of 13.2 overs; he bowled six maidens for eight runs and three wickets. May had three for 25, and Brown three for 35. At ten minutes past five W. Hartley and J. Bates came out on behalf of Stockport, the bowling being entrusted to Teather and Redfern. Bates cut Teather finely for four, while Hartley off-drove Redfern soon after for a similar number. Runs came at a good pace. With the score at 26 Bates was bowled by a ball from Teather which came several inches across, and removed his leg bail; that player had scored fourteen in good style. Arthur Torkington joined his captain, only to see him given out caught at the wicket, a decision which seemed doubtful. Brown came next, and here some of the finest cricket of the day was seen; both men hit out well. Still the bowling was good, but runs coming at a fair pace, Briggs displaced Teather when the score had reached 72. Torkington was bowled for a splendid innings of 24, which included three fours and four twos. F. Tyler came next, but after scoring three was bowled. H. Hartley came next, and the first ball he received he drove it magnificently right out off the ground for four...the big stroke quite taking the bowler by surprise. Sam Brown was out soon after from a big hit, which E. Rome took very well, after playing a grand game for 27, his innings included five fours and two twos. Disney came next and saw Hartley lay about both bowlers, Briggs coming in for a fair share of punishment, he getting that player away for two fours, but in attempting to repeat was caught at the wicket, his brilliant innings of 18 included three fours, one three, one two, and a single. He tried his very utmost to bring off a win, and indeed could he have stayed another quarter of an hour I believe his object would have been accomplished. Disney left at the same total, 103. With May and Selby in time was called, seven wickets being down for 110; thus the match ended in a draw in favour of the visitors. Below is appended full scores and analysis :-

MACCLESFIELD CHRONICLE: FRIDAY 30 AUGUST 1895 - 2

Stockport v. Cheadle Hulme.
Great interest was manifested in the meeting of these local rivals, which took place on the Cheadle Hulme ground in splendid weather and before a good attendance of spectators on Saturday afternoon. It was also set apart as the benefit of the Cheadle Hulme professional, Teather. The first match between these elevens this season ended in a very even draw. Skipper Hartley lost the toss, and of course had to field. C. V. Rome and Teather were the first batsmen to open the Cheadle innings to the bowling of Sam Brown and H May. Teather scored a single off Brown, but next over Harry May knocked his leg stump out of the ground. E Rome came next, and immediately cut Brown for four, while O Rome got May away for a like number, but was out soon after, Brown taking him well at short slip. 12-2-5. Briggs joined E. Rome, but with an addition of eight runs on Rome was finely caught by Hartley after scoring 10. A. Royle was next in, and saw Briggs get Brown away to leg for four, and next over repeated the stroke very luckily. Royle hit May for three, but soon after that player was magnificently caught by Harry May at short slip low down-a very fine catch indeed. C. Taylor joined Briggs, and here a stand took place, though Briggs did most scoring. At 62, however, Fred Torkington, who had displaced May, clean bowled Briggs, that player having made 27 in not a very taking fashion; thus half the wickets were down for 62. W. Redfern came next, but without any addition Fred Torkington upset his middle stump with one of his expresses. Taylor left at 67 and Rome at 69, while Kirk left at 71, there being nine wickets down. Then came a surprise for the Stockport men. Todd and Colles on getting together made things very lively for the fielders. Changes of bowling were tried, but to no use, the 100 going up amidst loud applause; both men played a fair game, Todd several times getting the ball away in nice style. W. Hartley went on with lobs, but caused no great sensation. When the total had reached 127 Colles was bowled by Fred Torkington, his innings of 15, coming at a time when things were looking serious, was of the utmost value to his side, while too much cannot be said of Todd, who carried out his bat for a splendid innings of 34. Certainly the Stockport men assisted them materially by their wretched fielding, but nevertheless they deserve great credit for their plucky performance. Fred Torkington came out with a splendid analysis out of 13.2 overs: he bowled six maidens for eight runs and three wickets. May had three for 25, and Brown three for 33. At ten minutes past five W. Hartley and J. Bates came out on behalf of Stockport, the bowling being entrusted to Teather and Redfern. Bates cut Teather finely for four, while Hartley off-drove Redfern soon after for a similar number. Runs came at a good pace. With the score at 24 Bates was bowled by a ball from Teather which came several inches across, and removed his leg bail; that player had scored fourteen in quick time. Arthur Torkington joined his captain, only to see him given out caught at the wicket, a decision which seemed doubtful. Brown came next, and here some of the finest cricket of the day was seen; both men hit out well. Still the bowling was good, but runs coming at a fair pace, Briggs displaced Teather when the score had reached 72. Torkington was bowled for a splendid innings of 24, which included three fours and four twos. F. Tyler came next, but after scoring three was bowled. H. Hartley came next, and the first ball he received he drove it magnificently right out off the ground for four, this stroke quite taking the bowler by surprise. Sam Brown was out soon after from a big hit, which E. Rome took very well, after playing a grand game for 27, his innings included five fours and two twos. Disney came next and saw Hartley lay about both bowlers, Briggs coming in for a fair share of punishment, he getting that player away for two fours, but in attempting to repeat was caught at the wicket, his brilliant innings of 18 included three fours, one three, one two, and a single. He tried his very utmost to bring off a win, and indeed could he have stayed another quarter of an hour I believe his object would have been accomplished. Disney left at the same total, 103. With May and Selby in time was called, seven wickets being down for 110; thus the match ended in a draw in favour of the visitors. Below is appended full scores and analysis :-

LIVERPOOL POST: FRIDAY 13 SEPTEMBER 1895: TUESDAY 17 SEPT 1895

LIVERPOOL CRICKET CLUB.

BATTING AND BOWLING AVERAGES.
The first eleven played 37 matches, won 16, lost 6, and 15 were drawn. Won: Western, Werneth (twice), Sefton (twice), Rossall School, Boughton Hall (twice), Bootle, Warrington, Huyton, Birkenhead Park, Preston, Rock Ferry, New Brighton, and Oxton. Lost: Huyton, [illegible] College, Manchester, Formby, and Leyland (twice). Drawn: Ormskirk (twice), Birkenhead Park, Marlborough College, Clifton College, Cheltenham College, Northern (twice), Oxton, Rock Ferry, Fettesian-Lorettonians, Harrow Wanderers, Western, Warrington, and Hoi Paparomenoi. Runs to the number of 6,951 (including 383 extras) were scored for the loss of 311 wickets, against 5,580 runs (including 322 extras) for the loss of 342 wickets. The "A" eleven played 16 matches, won 5, lost 4, and 7 were drawn, scoring 2,282 runs (including 144 extras) for the loss of 124 wickets, against 2,053 runs (including 177 extras) for the loss of 119 wickets. The second eleven played 27 matches, won 8, lost 7, and drew 12, scoring 3,878 runs (including 311 extras) for the loss of 204 wickets, against 3,897 runs (including 352 extras) for the loss of 324 wickets. The third eleven played 6 matches, of which 4 were won and 2 drawn. In all the club played 86 matches, of which 33 were won, 17 lost, and 36 drawn. District matches were played against Yorkshire County and Cambridge University, the former being won and the latter lost. In addition, 9 extra matches were played, giving a grand total of 97 matches during the season. Although Mr. A. T. Kemble has only taken part in 21 matches this season, he has succeeded in stumping and catching 41 opponents.

ST. AIDAN'S.
The past season has been a most successful one for this club. Of 19 matches played the team has won 17 losing two only, and one of those by the narrow majority of 7 runs. The total number of runs they are credited with is 1,497 for 179 wickets, against 593 for 190 wickets for opponents. The highest score obtained was 135 against Brunswick Y.M. Guild. Six times during the season was the century over-topped by the Aidanites, whilst the highest score made against them was 88. G. Lewis's 70 (not out) was the highest individual score for the Saints during the season.

W. H. Routledge. 237 ... 12 ... 90 ... 17 ... 450 ... 19·9

The First Eleven this year have done a little better than last. The averages show a big improvement.

A match between Liverpool and Oxton culminated in a win for Liverpool by 62 runs and 3 wickets. For Oxton, M. Field, H. S. Hibbert, and J. Curwen were the only double figurists in a score of 71.

Rock Ferry administered a severe thrashing to Cheadle Hulme. The Ferry batted first, and after scoring 126 for 9 wickets closed their innings, and their opponents, in reply, could only muster 32. The chief scorers for Rock Ferry were J. Ravenscroft (45) and C. Stobart (25).

New Brighton were once more obliged to knuckle under on Saturday, their conquerors this time being Formby. New Brighton, batting first, managed to amass 89 runs, chiefly through the aid of Moore (27), E. Drinkwater (11), and F. Pepper (10), these being the only New Brightonians who succeeded in reaching double figures. Formby showed to much better advantage, as they managed to compile 108 for 7 wickets.

Wallasey took on the second string of Oxton, on the latter's ground, and succeeded in defeating them by 20 runs, J. C. Kay (19) and G. Turner (18) being chiefly instrumental in securing Oxton's downfall.

Bootle secured an easy victory over Birkenhead Victoria, the scene of the conflict being Bootle. The Victorians batted first and scored 71, of which F. Wright compiled 22 (not out), Bootle replying with 126 for 6 wickets.

Another easy victory was that of Bromboro' over Spital, whom they swamped to the tune of 61 with 4 wickets to spare. For Bromboro' the chief scorers were found in J. Pugh with 50, and H. Pugh with 36, while for the losers W. Bowring and G. Earl were first with 22 and 11 respectively.

Tranmere Wesley overcame West Derby 2nd by sixteen runs, J. Turnbull (12) being the only double figurist in the match. Buckley and Quinn in the bowling department took 4 wickets each at a cost of thirteen runs.

Y.M.C.A. finished a generally successful season by defeating Ravenscroft by the substantial majority of 61, E. R. Davies, R. Willacey, and L. Bunting showing good form with the bat for scores of 26, 12, and 11 respectively.

FIXTURES FOR TO-DAY.

Birkenhead News : SATURDAY 14 SEPTEMBER 1895 - 2.

CRICKET NOTES.

Saturday saw the practical conclusion of the season, the leading local clubs all finishing their list of engagements. The Premier club should have played Huyton, but as the latter's ground was nothing short of a quagmire after the heavy rain of the previous evening, the match had perforce to be abandoned. It is some time back now since the Parkites have had such a successful season as the one just concluded, as their position at the head of the district competition readily testifies. Independent, however, of their excellent record against the district clubs, they have done more than well against such powerful opponents as those of Manchester, Leyland and Wigan, the latter being beaten in both their engagements with the Park club. It will be seen on reference to the batting and bowling averages that T. R. Hubback heads the list in both departments of the game, a position which indicates what an invaluable acquisition he has been to his club with both bat and ball, independent of the great service he has rendered behind the wicket. R. Fielding Ould has secured the second position in the batting list, being closely followed by H. J. Fish, who, owing to a severe illness has been prevented from taking part in several important matches. On the whole skipper Holden can hardly be said to have played up to the form exhibited by him for several previous years, and it looks a trifle curious to see his name so low down in the list as fourth, but still an average of 31·11 for 26 innings is by no means bad, and besides this he has rendered excellent service with the ball, having captured 45 wickets at an average cost of 9·20. Indeed he has seldom bowled better than he has done this season, and he is to be congratulated on his capital record with the sphere. H. M. Grayson comes out fairly well with an average of 19·1 for 16 innings, but Smith shows a great falling off as compared with last year, his figures being 16·3 for 19 ventures. Town has done a little better, he having an average of 18 for 13 innings, whilst others who figure rather low down, but who may be confidently expected to do much better in the future, include H. W. P. Bennette, H. Salmon, and H. S. Cochrane. That good old stonewaller, A. B. Wilson, does not appear to have exercised his usual discretion, for he comes very near to being the "absolute," this unenviable position being occupied by Captain Byrne. The brunt of the bowling has of course been borne by Smith, who has taken 88 wickets for 10·9, whilst C. E. Steen's 48 have been secured at a rate of 13·7. Town has had a singularly unfortunate season, as although he has bowled uniformly well he has been able to get but few wickets, 34 at a cost of 22·11 being rather expensive.

The Rock Ferryites concluded a fairly successful season on Saturday with a win over Cheadle Hulme. Of the 25 matches they have played, 11 have been won, 6 drawn, and 7 lost, whilst one had the almost unique result of a tie. A glance at the batting averages shows E. F. Kearsey at the head of affairs with the nice figures of 30·88 for nine innings, and it seems rather a pity that his services are not more often available for first team matches. A. J. Cripps comes next, but his average compares unfavourable with that of Kearsey, there being a considerable difference between 30·88 and 23, albeit Cripps has played two more innings. With the exception of his captain, A. Stott, who is placed third, has taken part in more matches than any other player in the team, and his 21·13 for 24 innings must be set down as distinctly creditable. G. G. Paton has an average of 20·4 for 14 innings, whilst immediately following him comes "Captain" Ravenscroft who, on the whole, has been far from fortunate, repeatedly failures reducing his figures to as low as 19·79 for 26 innings. Another batsman who has not shown to great advantage this season is T. R. Bretherton, whose figures of 18·78 for 20 attempts are not as good as might be expected from such a capable willow wielder, whilst the average of A. C. Blain is also a little disappointing, this usually remarkably vigorous batsman being comparatively low down in the list with 18·33 for 18 innings. Needham's figures are 17·28 for 23 attempts, the next in order of merit being A. C. Bamford, who for 16 innings has an average of 14·46. Surprisingly low down is H. S. Bamford who, however, has not devoted much time to the game, having only been at the wickets on six occasions, for which he has an average of 10·14. J. Bretherton, E. Stott, G. B. Paterson, and E. C. Rylands are also allotted places in the list, the last being Ryland who, out of 14 innings, has been not out no less than seven times, and would probably have had a much higher average than 4·14 if he had been given a chance of showing his abilities with the bat. If he has not proved himself a brilliant batsman, however, there can be no question about his success with the ball, for during the season he has captured no fewer than 113 wickets at the moderate rate of 11·03 per wicket. J. Bretherton, though bowling well, falls far behind Ryland, the fast bowler only crediting himself with 33 wickets at a cost of 12·36. With such strong amateur bowling talent at its disposal, the club was not required to call upon the services of Needham to any great extent, but the pro has been very useful at times, and has secured 25 wickets at an average cost of 17·19.

Though the Oxtonites have been under a cloud for the last two or three weeks, they cannot be said to have fared badly on the whole, as out of 28 matches played they have won 9 and drawn 13, being absolutely beaten on but five occasions, one, the match referred to in my remarks about Rock Ferry, resulting in a tie. On Saturday, at Aigburth, they met with another serious reverse, receiving a shocking trouncing at the hands of the Liverpool representatives, who disposed of them for 71, and knocked up 133 for the loss of seven wickets. The Oxtonites thus ended the season with three successive losses. Their batting averages reveal the fact that M. Court has not only scored the most runs, but he has also the best average, his figures being 32·67 for 27 innings, a really excellent record for this very promising young player. The second position on the list is filled by J. H. F. Grayson, with 27·87 for nine innings, but now that this undoubtedly fine batsman has settled down and definitely thrown in his lot with the Oxtonites, we may expect to see a marked improvement in his figures next year. Another old Park player takes third honours, this being W. Turner, who has developed into one of the mainstays of the Oxton batting, this being evident by his excellent average of 25·67 for 25 innings. The Oxton skipper, C. F. Hutton has been a little off colour occasionally during the season, and has not scored quite so prolifically as he has done in former years, his highest contribution in an innings being 52, and his average 20·38 for 15 ventures. That the new pro. Bishop will be a great acquisition to the Oxtonites next season there can be but little doubt, for during the short time he has been engaged he has done wonderfully well with both bat and ball, having a batting average of 19·29 for 9 innings, whilst with the ball he has secured 48 wickets at a cost of 9·4 per wicket. J. Curwen has scored better and more consistently than he has done for a considerable time, and his record of 19·12 for 20 innings indicates that his powers as a batsman have not yet altogether left him. H. T. Hibbert has only taken part in 12 matches, for which his figures are 16·17, whilst for 19 innings M. Field has an average of 14·22. Through an injury received early on in the season, Smith was unable to take as active a part in the campaign as could be wished, and for his 12 innings he is rather low down on the list with 13·52. H. Bickersteth has played in ten matches, and has scarcely been so successful in taking out his bat as he has been in former seasons, consequently his average has suffered somewhat, and his figures of 10·86 are not calculated to make him unduly conceited. G. H. Court, B. S. Smith, and P. L. Bromnell are all close together with figures ranging from 10·33 to 8·18, L. Court bringing up the rear with 3·86 for 2 innings. Bowling honours are divided between amateurs and professionals, Bishop coming out well with 41 wickets at a cost of 9.4. Smith has proved a trifle more expensive his figures being 63 for 9·62, but his record is an excellent one for all that, more especially in view of the accident he met with, before referred to. G. H. Court has also rendered splendid service with the ball, capturing no less than 49 wickets, whilst his younger brother L. Court has given signs of future usefulness by securing 12 wickets for 3·86 per wicket.

Like that of Oxton, the Birkenhead Victoria first string have been faring badly of late, and also like Oxton they concluded their campaign on Saturday with a disastrous defeat, the Bootle representatives, who have done anything but well themselves this year, achieving an easy victory over the evidently disorganised Victorites, who made a very feeble show with the bat, and were all sent to the rightabout for 71. On the whole the Victoria representatives can hardly be congratulated on their season's record, for out of 25 matches played they have only won four, whilst 9 have been lost and 12 drawn. It is not surprising to see J. Wylde at the head of the batting averages with 25·14, for he has shown consistent good form throughout the season. Others who may be said to have done well are W. T. Davies (22·14), F. Wright (19·4), W. H. Routledge (18·7) and J. Macdonald (17·3), but a falling off is noticeable in the batting of J. Brocklebank, who is very low down on the list with 14·6, though he has played in more matches than any of his colleagues. J. Fish has been a distinct acquisition with the leather and heads the bowling averages with 27 wickets for a trifle over 11 runs per wicket. So far as taking wickets is concerned, however, J. Brocklebank is an easy first, he having captured 42 at a cost of 14·32. Several others of the team have had a trial with the ball, these including J. West, S. Wright, M. Sprat, J. Wylde, E. H. Carter, and W. H. Routledge.
